<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Coriolis force</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_effect&amp;redirect=no" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>)</span></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the psychophysical perception effect, see <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect_(perception)" title="Coriolis effect (perception)">Coriolis effect (perception)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corioliskraftanimation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="283" data-file-width="200" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Corioliskraftanimation.gif" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">In the inertial frame of reference (upper part of the picture), the black ball moves in a straight line. However, the observer (brown dot) who is standing in the rotating/non-inertial frame of reference (lower part of the picture) sees the object as following a curved path due to the Coriolis and centrifugal forces present in this frame.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, the <b>Coriolis force</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Fictitious_force" title="Fictitious force">inertial force</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> that acts on objects that are in motion relative to a <a href="/wiki/Rotating_reference_frame" title="Rotating reference frame">rotating reference frame</a>. In a reference frame with <a href="/wiki/Clockwise" title="Clockwise">clockwise</a> rotation, the force acts to the left of the motion of the object. In one with anticlockwise rotation, the force acts to the right. <a href="/wiki/Deflection_(physics)" title="Deflection (physics)">Deflection</a> of an object due to the Coriolis force is called the <b>Coriolis effect</b>. Though recognized previously by others, the mathematical expression for the Coriolis force appeared in an 1835 paper by French scientist <a href="/wiki/Gaspard-Gustave_de_Coriolis" title="Gaspard-Gustave de Coriolis">Gaspard-Gustave de Coriolis</a>, in connection with the theory of <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheels</a>. Early in the 20th century, the term <i>Coriolis force</i> began to be used in connection with <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's laws of motion</a> describe the motion of an object in an <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">inertial (non-accelerating) frame of reference</a>. When Newton's laws are transformed to a rotating frame of reference, the Coriolis force and <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> appear. Both forces are proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of the object. The Coriolis force is proportional to the rotation rate and the centrifugal force is proportional to its square. The Coriolis force acts in a direction perpendicular to the rotation axis and to the velocity of the body in the rotating frame and is proportional to the object's speed in the rotating frame (more precisely, to the component of its velocity that is perpendicular to the axis of rotation). The centrifugal force acts outwards in the radial direction and is proportional to the distance of the body from the axis of the rotating frame. These additional forces are termed inertial forces, <a href="/wiki/Fictitious_force" title="Fictitious force">fictitious forces</a> or <i>pseudo forces</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> They allow the application of Newton's laws to a rotating system. They are correction factors that do not exist in a non-accelerating or inertial reference frame.</p>
<p>In popular (non-technical) usage of the term "Coriolis effect", the rotating reference frame implied is almost always the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. Because the Earth spins, Earth-bound observers need to account for the Coriolis force to correctly analyze the motion of objects. The Earth completes one rotation per day, so for motions of everyday objects the Coriolis force is usually quite small compared to other forces; its effects generally become noticeable only for motions occurring over large distances and long periods of time, such as large-scale movement of air in the atmosphere or water in the ocean. Such motions are constrained by the surface of the Earth, so only the horizontal component of the Coriolis force is generally important. This force causes moving objects on the surface of the Earth to be deflected to the right (with respect to the direction of travel) in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> and to the left in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>. The horizontal deflection effect is greater near the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">poles</a>, since the effective rotation rate about a local vertical axis is largest there, and decreases to zero at the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Rather than flowing directly from areas of high pressure to low pressure, as they would in a non-rotating system, winds and currents tend to flow to the right of this direction north of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> and to the left of this direction south of it. This effect is responsible for the rotation of large <a href="/wiki/Cyclone#Structure" title="Cyclone">cyclones</a> (see <a href="#Meteorology">Coriolis effects in meteorology</a>).</p>
<p>For an intuitive explanation of the origin of the Coriolis force, consider an object moving northward in the northern hemisphere. Viewed from outer space, the object does not appear to go due north, but has an eastward motion (it rotates around toward the right along with the surface of the Earth). The further north you go, the smaller the "horizontal diameter" of the Earth, and so the slower the eastward motion of its surface. As the object moves north, to higher latitudes, it has a tendency to maintain the eastward speed it started with (rather than slowing down to match the reduced eastward speed of local objects on the Earth's surface), so it veers east (i.e. to the right of its initial motion).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Though not obvious from this example, which considers northward motion, the horizontal deflection occurs equally for objects moving east or west (or any other direction).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dechales-Coriolis-Cannon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="395" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Dechales-Coriolis-Cannon.jpg/220px-Dechales-Coriolis-Cannon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Dechales-Coriolis-Cannon.jpg/330px-Dechales-Coriolis-Cannon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Dechales-Coriolis-Cannon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dechales-Coriolis-Cannon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Image from <i>Cursus seu Mundus Mathematicus</i> (1674) of C.F.M. Dechales, showing how a cannonball should deflect to the right of its target on a rotating Earth, because the rightward motion of the ball is faster than that of the tower.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dechales-Coriolis-Tower.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="341" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Dechales-Coriolis-Tower.jpg/220px-Dechales-Coriolis-Tower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Dechales-Coriolis-Tower.jpg/330px-Dechales-Coriolis-Tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/50/Dechales-Coriolis-Tower.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dechales-Coriolis-Tower.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Image from <i>Cursus seu Mundus Mathematicus</i> (1674) of C.F.M. Dechales, showing how a ball should fall from a tower on a rotating Earth. The ball is released from <i>F</i>. The top of the tower moves faster than its base, so while the ball falls, the base of the tower moves to <i>I</i>, but the ball, which has the eastward speed of the tower's top, outruns the tower's base and lands further to the east at <i>L</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Italian scientist <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Riccioli" title="Giovanni Battista Riccioli">Giovanni Battista Riccioli</a> and his assistant <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Maria_Grimaldi" title="Francesco Maria Grimaldi">Francesco Maria Grimaldi</a> described the effect in connection with artillery in the 1651 <i>Almagestum Novum</i>, writing that rotation of the Earth should cause a cannonball fired to the north to deflect to the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1674 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claude_Fran%C3%A7ois_Milliet_Dechales" title="Claude François Milliet Dechales">Claude François Milliet Dechales</a> described in his <i>Cursus seu Mundus Mathematicus</i> how the rotation of the earth should cause a deflection in the trajectories of both falling bodies and projectiles aimed toward one of the planet's poles. Riccioli, Grimaldi, and Dechales all described the effect as part of an argument against the heliocentric system of Copernicus. In other words, they argued that the Earth's rotation should create the effect, and so failure to detect the effect was evidence for an immobile Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The Coriolis acceleration equation was derived by Euler in 1749<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and the effect was described in the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_tides" title="Theory of tides">tidal equations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Pierre-Simon Laplace</a> in 1778.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cartwright2000_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cartwright2000-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaspard-Gustave_Coriolis" title="Gaspard-Gustave Coriolis">Gaspard-Gustave Coriolis</a> published a paper in 1835 on the energy yield of machines with rotating parts, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterwheel" title="Waterwheel">waterwheels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corps_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-corps-12">[12]</a></sup> That paper considered the supplementary forces that are detected in a rotating frame of reference. Coriolis divided these supplementary forces into two categories. The second category contained a force that arises from the <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">cross product</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">angular velocity</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_system" title="Coordinate system">coordinate system</a> and the projection of a particle's <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> into a plane <a href="/wiki/Perpendicular" title="Perpendicular">perpendicular</a> to the system's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_of_rotation" title="Axis of rotation">axis of rotation</a>. Coriolis referred to this force as the "compound centrifugal force" due to its analogies with the <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> already considered in category one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The effect was known in the early 20th century as the "<a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> of Coriolis",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and by 1920 as "Coriolis force".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1856, <a href="/wiki/William_Ferrel" title="William Ferrel">William Ferrel</a> proposed the existence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrel_cell" title="Ferrel cell">circulation cell</a> in the mid-latitudes with air being deflected by the Coriolis force to create the <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">prevailing westerly winds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The understanding of the kinematics of how exactly the rotation of the Earth affects airflow was partial at first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Late in the 19th century, the full extent of the large scale interaction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_gradient_force" title="Pressure gradient force">pressure gradient force</a> and deflecting force that in the end causes air masses to move 'along' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isobar_(meteorology)" title="Isobar (meteorology)">isobars</a> was understood.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formula">Formula</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Formula">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fictitious_force" title="Fictitious force">Fictitious force</a></div>
<p>In non-vector terms: at a given rate of rotation of the observer, the magnitude of the Coriolis acceleration of the object is proportional to the velocity of the object and also to the sine of the angle between the direction of movement of the object and the axis of rotation.</p>
<p>The vector formula for the magnitude and direction of the Coriolis acceleration is derived through vector analysis and is<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coffin_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coffin-19">[19]</a></sup></p>

<p>The equation may be multiplied by the mass of the relevant object to produce the Coriolis force:</p>

</dl>
<p>See <i><a href="/wiki/Fictitious_force" title="Fictitious force">fictitious force</a></i> for a derivation.</p>
<p>The <i>Coriolis effect</i> is the behavior added by the <i>Coriolis acceleration</i>. The formula implies that the Coriolis acceleration is perpendicular both to the direction of the velocity of the moving mass and to the frame's rotation axis. So in particular:</p>
<ul>
<li>if the velocity is parallel to the rotation axis, the Coriolis acceleration is zero. (For example, on Earth, this situation occurs for a body on the equator moving north or south relative to Earth's surface.)</li>
<li>if the velocity is straight inward to the axis, the acceleration is in the direction of local rotation. (For example, on Earth, this situation occurs for a body on the equator falling downward, as in the Dechales illustration above, where the falling ball travels further to the east than does the tower.)</li>
<li>if the velocity is straight outward from the axis, the acceleration is against the direction of local rotation. (In the tower example, a ball launched upward would move toward the west.)</li>
<li>if the velocity is in the direction of local rotation, the acceleration is outward from the axis. (For example, on Earth, this situation occurs for a body on the equator moving east relative to Earth's surface. It would move upward as seen by an observer on the surface. This effect (see Eötvös effect below) was discussed by Galileo Galilei in 1632 and by Riccioli in 1651.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graney2015_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graney2015-20">[20]</a></sup>)</li>
<li>if the velocity is against the direction of local rotation, the acceleration is inward to the axis. (On Earth, this situation occurs for a body on the equator moving west, which would deflect downward as seen by an observer.)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Coriolis force exists only when one uses a rotating reference frame. In the rotating frame it behaves exactly like a real force (that is to say, it causes acceleration and has real effects). However, the Coriolis force is a consequence of <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and is not attributable to an identifiable originating body, as is the case for electromagnetic or nuclear forces, for example. From an analytical viewpoint, to use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_second_law" title="Newton's second law">Newton's second law</a> in a rotating system, the Coriolis force is mathematically necessary, but it disappears in a non-accelerating, inertial frame of reference. For example, consider two children on opposite sides of a spinning roundabout (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merry-go-round" title="Merry-go-round">Merry-go-round</a> ), who are throwing a ball to each other. From the children's point of view, this ball's path is curved sideways by the Coriolis force. Suppose the roundabout spins anticlockwise when viewed from above. From the thrower's perspective, the deflection is to the right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CalvinandHobbs_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-CalvinandHobbs-22">[22]</a></sup> From the non-thrower's perspective, deflection is to the left. <i>For a mathematical formulation see <a href="/wiki/Fictitious_force#Mathematical_derivation_of_fictitious_forces" title="Fictitious force">Mathematical derivation of fictitious forces</a>.</i> In meteorology, a rotating frame (the Earth) with its Coriolis force provides a more natural framework for explanation of air movements than a non-rotating, inertial frame without Coriolis forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barry0_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barry0-23">[23]</a></sup> In long-range gunnery, sight corrections for the Earth's rotation are based upon the Coriolis force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor0_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor0-24">[24]</a></sup> These examples are described in more detail below.</p>
<p>The acceleration entering the Coriolis force arises from two sources of change in velocity that result from rotation: the first is the change of the velocity of an object in time. The same velocity (in an inertial frame of reference where the normal laws of physics apply) is seen as different velocities at different times in a rotating frame of reference. The apparent acceleration is proportional to the angular velocity of the reference frame (the rate at which the coordinate axes change direction), and to the component of velocity of the object in a plane perpendicular to the axis of rotation. This gives a term <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">Ω<!-- Ω --></mi>
</mrow>
<mo>×<!-- × --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold-italic">v</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle -{\boldsymbol {\Omega }}\times {\boldsymbol {v}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="-{\boldsymbol {\Omega }}\times {\boldsymbol {v}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ae5e2a5a9c803f044013de6868bf86f086ae11e3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.94ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. The minus sign arises from the traditional definition of the cross product (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_hand_rule" title="Right hand rule">right hand rule</a>), and from the sign convention for angular velocity vectors.</p>
<p>The second is the change of velocity in space. Different positions in a rotating frame of reference have different velocities (as seen from an inertial frame of reference). For an object to move in a straight line, it must accelerate so that its velocity changes from point to point by the same amount as the velocities of the frame of reference. The force is proportional to the angular velocity (which determines the relative speed of two different points in the rotating frame of reference), and to the component of the velocity of the object in a plane perpendicular to the axis of rotation (which determines how quickly it moves between those points). This also gives a term <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">Ω<!-- Ω --></mi>
</mrow>
<mo>×<!-- × --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold-italic">v</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle -{\boldsymbol {\Omega }}\times {\boldsymbol {v}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="-{\boldsymbol {\Omega }}\times {\boldsymbol {v}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ae5e2a5a9c803f044013de6868bf86f086ae11e3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.94ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Length_scales_and_the_Rossby_number">Length scales and the Rossby number</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Length scales and the Rossby number">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Rossby_number" title="Rossby number">Rossby number</a></div>
<p>The time, space and velocity scales are important in determining the importance of the Coriolis force. Whether rotation is important in a system can be determined by its <a href="/wiki/Rossby_number" title="Rossby number">Rossby number</a>, which is the ratio of the velocity, <i>U</i>, of a system to the product of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_parameter" title="Coriolis parameter">Coriolis parameter</a>,<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f=2\omega \sin \varphi \,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f=2\omega \sin \varphi \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2154826491cca66019774e92fcb7bff188354d37" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:12.606ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>, and the length scale, <i>L</i>, of the motion:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>R</mi>
<mi>o</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow>
<mi>f</mi>
<mi>L</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Ro={\frac {U}{fL}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Ro={\frac {U}{fL}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5d7f5ad6a6081dd39d219dc58df6fc42a6979bfd" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:10.397ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The Rossby number is the ratio of inertial to Coriolis forces. A small Rossby number indicates a system is strongly affected by Coriolis forces, and a large Rossby number indicates a system in which inertial forces dominate. For example, in tornadoes, the Rossby number is large, in low-pressure systems it is low, and in oceanic systems it is around 1. As a result, in tornadoes the Coriolis force is negligible, and balance is between pressure and centrifugal forces. In low-pressure systems, centrifugal force is negligible and balance is between Coriolis and pressure forces. In the oceans all three forces are comparable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakshmi_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakshmi-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>An atmospheric system moving at <i>U</i> = 10 m/s (22 mph) occupying a spatial distance of <i>L</i> = 1,000 km (621 mi), has a Rossby number of approximately 0.1.</p>
<p>A baseball pitcher may throw the ball at U = 45 m/s (100 mph) for a distance of L = 18.3 m (60 ft). The Rossby number in this case would be 32,000.</p>
<p>Baseball players don't care about which hemisphere they're playing in. However, an unguided missile obeys exactly the same physics as a baseball, but can travel far enough and be in the air long enough to experience the effect of Coriolis force. Long-range shells in the Northern Hemisphere landed close to, but to the right of, where they were aimed until this was noted. (Those fired in the Southern Hemisphere landed to the left.) In fact, it was this effect that first got the attention of Coriolis himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Butz_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Butz-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holton_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holton-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_cases">Simple cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Simple cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cannon_on_turntable">Cannon on turntable</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cannon on turntable">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-technical" role="presentation">

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Target_on_turntable.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="607" data-file-width="1237" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Target_on_turntable.PNG/300px-Target_on_turntable.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Target_on_turntable.PNG/450px-Target_on_turntable.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Target_on_turntable.PNG/600px-Target_on_turntable.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Target_on_turntable.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cannon at the center of a rotating turntable. To hit the target located at position 1 on the perimeter at time <i>t</i> = 0 s, the cannon must be aimed ahead of the target at angle θ. That way, by the time the cannonball reaches position 3 on the periphery, the target is also at that position. In an inertial frame of reference, the cannonball travels a straight radial path to the target (curve <i>y</i><sub>A</sub>). However, in the frame of the turntable, the path is arched (curve <i>y</i><sub>B</sub>), as also shown in the figure.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trajectory_for_three_angles_of_launch.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="363" data-file-width="586" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Trajectory_for_three_angles_of_launch.PNG/300px-Trajectory_for_three_angles_of_launch.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Trajectory_for_three_angles_of_launch.PNG/450px-Trajectory_for_three_angles_of_launch.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Trajectory_for_three_angles_of_launch.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trajectory_for_three_angles_of_launch.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Successful trajectory of cannonball as seen from the turntable for three angles of launch θ. Plotted points are for the same equally spaced times steps on each curve. Cannonball speed <i>v</i> is held constant and angular rate of rotation ω is varied to achieve a successful "hit" for selected θ. For example, for a radius of 1 m and a cannonball speed of 1 m/s, the time of flight <i>t</i><sub><i>f</i></sub> = 1 s, and <i>ωt</i><sub><i>f</i></sub> = <i>θ</i> → <i>ω</i> and <i>θ</i> have the same numerical value if <i>θ</i> is expressed in radians. The wider spacing of the plotted points as the target is approached show the speed of the cannonball is accelerating as seen on the turntable, due to fictitious Coriolis and centrifugal forces.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vector_relationships.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="665" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Vector_relationships.PNG/300px-Vector_relationships.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Vector_relationships.PNG/450px-Vector_relationships.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Vector_relationships.PNG/600px-Vector_relationships.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vector_relationships.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Acceleration components at an earlier time (top) and at arrival time at the target (bottom)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cannon_force_components.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="823" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cannon_force_components.PNG/300px-Cannon_force_components.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cannon_force_components.PNG/450px-Cannon_force_components.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cannon_force_components.PNG/600px-Cannon_force_components.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cannon_force_components.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coriolis acceleration, centrifugal acceleration and net acceleration vectors at three selected points on the trajectory as seen on the turntable.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The animation at the top of this article is a classic illustration of Coriolis force. Another visualization of the Coriolis and centrifugal forces is <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=49JwbrXcPjc" rel="nofollow">this animation clip</a>.</p>
<p>Given the radius <i>R</i> of the turntable in that animation, the rate of angular rotation ω, and the speed of the cannonball (assumed constant) <i>v</i>, the correct angle θ to aim so as to hit the target at the edge of the turntable can be calculated.</p>
<p>The inertial frame of reference provides one way to handle the question: calculate the time to interception, which is <i>t<sub>f</sub></i> = <i>R</i> / <i>v</i> . Then, the turntable revolves an angle ω <i>t<sub>f</sub></i> in this time. If the cannon is pointed an angle θ = ω <i>t<sub>f</sub></i> = ω <i>R</i> / <i>v</i>, then the cannonball arrives at the periphery at position number 3 at the same time as the target.</p>
<p>No discussion of Coriolis force can arrive at this solution as simply, so the reason to treat this problem is to demonstrate Coriolis formalism in an easily visualized situation.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trajectory_in_the_inertial_frame">Trajectory in the inertial frame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Trajectory in the inertial frame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The trajectory in the inertial frame (denoted <i>A</i>) is a straight line radial path at angle θ. The position of the cannonball in (<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>) coordinates at time <i>t</i> is:</p>

<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Accelerations">Accelerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Accelerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>

</math></span><img alt="-2\omega ^{2}\mathbf {r_{B}} (t)\ ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b58f3cdbe32e3fe70fd5475adee09abce7b62bd3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:12.124ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>These accelerations are shown in the diagrams for a particular example.</p>
<p>It is seen that the Coriolis acceleration not only cancels the centrifugal acceleration, but together they provide a net "centripetal", radially inward component of acceleration (that is, directed toward the center of rotation):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caution_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-caution-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>and an additional component of acceleration perpendicular to <b>r<sub>B</sub></b> <i>(t)</i>:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {a_{C\perp }} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbf {a_{C\perp }} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7bb1875aadbb10b6c82e0a821fcd9f7973201103" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.201ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle =2\omega v\left(\sin \alpha ,\ -\cos \alpha \right)\ .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="=2\omega v\left(\sin \alpha ,\ -\cos \alpha \right)\ ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bd18ccb70e5f723810940da5606a7eba8a49c2fd" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:24.349ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The "centripetal" component of acceleration resembles that for <a href="/wiki/Circular_motion" title="Circular motion">circular motion</a> at radius <i>r</i><sub>B</sub>, while the perpendicular component is velocity dependent, increasing with the radial velocity <i>v</i> and directed to the right of the velocity. The situation could be described as a circular motion combined with an "apparent Coriolis acceleration" of 2ω<i>v</i>. However, this is a rough labelling: a careful designation of the true centripetal force refers to a <a href="/wiki/Centripetal_force#Local_coordinates" title="Centripetal force">local reference frame</a> that employs the directions normal and tangential to the path, not coordinates referred to the axis of rotation.</p>
<p>These results also can be obtained directly by two time differentiations of <b>r<sub>B</sub></b> <i>(t)</i>. Agreement of the two approaches demonstrates that one could start from the general expression for fictitious acceleration above and derive the trajectories shown here. However, working from the acceleration to the trajectory is more complicated than the reverse procedure used here, which, of course, is made possible in this example by knowing the answer in advance.</p>
<p>As a result of this analysis an important point appears: <i>all</i> the fictitious accelerations must be included to obtain the correct trajectory. In particular, besides the Coriolis acceleration, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force_(rotating_reference_frame)" title="Centrifugal force (rotating reference frame)">centrifugal force</a> plays an essential role. It is easy to get the impression from verbal discussions of the cannonball problem, which focus on displaying the Coriolis effect particularly, that the Coriolis force is the only factor that must be considered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-30">[30]</a></sup> but that is not so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tavel_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tavel-31">[31]</a></sup> A turntable for which the Coriolis force <i>is</i> the only factor is the <a href="#parabolic_turntable">parabolic turntable</a>. A somewhat more complex situation is the idealized example of flight routes over long distances, where the centrifugal force of the path and <a href="/wiki/Lift_(force)" title="Lift (force)">aeronautical lift</a> are countered by <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">gravitational attraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ogden_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ogden-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCully2_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCully2-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tossed_ball_on_a_rotating_carousel">Tossed ball on a rotating carousel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tossed ball on a rotating carousel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coriolis_construction.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="548" data-file-width="965" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Coriolis_construction.JPG/300px-Coriolis_construction.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Coriolis_construction.JPG/450px-Coriolis_construction.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Coriolis_construction.JPG/600px-Coriolis_construction.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coriolis_construction.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A carousel is rotating counter-clockwise. <i>Left panel</i>: a ball is tossed by a thrower at 12:00 o'clock and travels in a straight line to the center of the carousel. While it travels, the thrower circles in a counter-clockwise direction. <i>Right panel</i>: The ball's motion as seen by the thrower, who now remains at 12:00 o'clock, because there is no rotation from their viewpoint.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The figure illustrates a ball tossed from 12:00 o'clock toward the center of a counter-clockwise rotating carousel. On the left, the ball is seen by a stationary observer above the carousel, and the ball travels in a straight line to the center, while the ball-thrower rotates counter-clockwise with the carousel. On the right the ball is seen by an observer rotating with the carousel, so the ball-thrower appears to stay at 12:00 o'clock. The figure shows how the trajectory of the ball as seen by the rotating observer can be constructed.</p>
<p>On the left, two arrows locate the ball relative to the ball-thrower. One of these arrows is from the thrower to the center of the carousel (providing the ball-thrower's line of sight), and the other points from the center of the carousel to the ball.(This arrow gets shorter as the ball approaches the center.) A shifted version of the two arrows is shown dotted.</p>
<p>On the right is shown this same dotted pair of arrows, but now the pair are rigidly rotated so the arrow corresponding to the line of sight of the ball-thrower toward the center of the carousel is aligned with 12:00 o'clock. The other arrow of the pair locates the ball relative to the center of the carousel, providing the position of the ball as seen by the rotating observer. By following this procedure for several positions, the trajectory in the rotating frame of reference is established as shown by the curved path in the right-hand panel.</p>
<p>The ball travels in the air, and there is no net force upon it. To the stationary observer the ball follows a straight-line path, so there is no problem squaring this trajectory with zero net force. However, the rotating observer sees a <i>curved</i> path. Kinematics insists that a force (pushing to the <i>right</i> of the instantaneous direction of travel for a <i>counter-clockwise</i> rotation) must be present to cause this curvature, so the rotating observer is forced to invoke a combination of centrifugal and Coriolis forces to provide the net force required to cause the curved trajectory.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bounced_ball">Bounced ball</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Bounced ball">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Points_of_view.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="1044" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Points_of_view.PNG/300px-Points_of_view.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Points_of_view.PNG/450px-Points_of_view.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Points_of_view.PNG/600px-Points_of_view.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Points_of_view.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bird's-eye view of carousel. The carousel rotates clockwise. Two viewpoints are illustrated: that of the camera at the center of rotation rotating with the carousel (left panel) and that of the inertial (stationary) observer (right panel). Both observers agree at any given time just how far the ball is from the center of the carousel, but not on its orientation. Time intervals are 1/10 of time from launch to bounce.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The figure describes a more complex situation where the tossed ball on a turntable bounces off the edge of the carousel and then returns to the tosser, who catches the ball. The effect of Coriolis force on its trajectory is shown again as seen by two observers: an observer (referred to as the "camera") that rotates with the carousel, and an inertial observer. The figure shows a bird's-eye view based upon the same ball speed on forward and return paths. Within each circle, plotted dots show the same time points. In the left panel, from the camera's viewpoint at the center of rotation, the tosser (smiley face) and the rail both are at fixed locations, and the ball makes a very considerable arc on its travel toward the rail, and takes a more direct route on the way back. From the ball tosser's viewpoint, the ball seems to return more quickly than it went (because the tosser is rotating toward the ball on the return flight).</p>
<p>On the carousel, instead of tossing the ball straight at a rail to bounce back, the tosser must throw the ball toward the right of the target and the ball then seems to the camera to bear continuously to the left of its direction of travel to hit the rail (<i>left</i> because the carousel is turning <i>clockwise</i>). The ball appears to bear to the left from direction of travel on both inward and return trajectories. The curved path demands this observer to recognize a leftward net force on the ball. (This force is "fictitious" because it disappears for a stationary observer, as is discussed shortly.) For some angles of launch, a path has portions where the trajectory is approximately radial, and Coriolis force is primarily responsible for the apparent deflection of the ball (centrifugal force is radial from the center of rotation, and causes little deflection on these segments). When a path curves away from radial, however, centrifugal force contributes significantly to deflection.</p>
<p>The ball's path through the air is straight when viewed by observers standing on the ground (right panel). In the right panel (stationary observer), the ball tosser (smiley face) is at 12 o'clock and the rail the ball bounces from is at position one (1). From the inertial viewer's standpoint, positions one (1), two (2), three (3) are occupied in sequence. At position 2 the ball strikes the rail, and at position 3 the ball returns to the tosser. Straight-line paths are followed because the ball is in free flight, so this observer requires that no net force is applied.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applied_to_the_Earth">Applied to the Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Applied to the Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>An important case where the Coriolis force is observed is the rotating Earth. Unless otherwise stated, directions of forces and motion apply to the Northern Hemisphere.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intuitive_explanation">Intuitive explanation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Intuitive explanation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As the Earth turns around its axis, everything attached to it turns with it (imperceptibly to our senses). An object that is moving without being dragged along with this rotation travels in a straight motion over the turning Earth. From our rotating perspective on the planet, its direction of motion changes as it moves, bending in the opposite direction to our actual motion. When viewed from a stationary point in space directly above the north pole, any land feature in the Northern Hemisphere turns anticlockwise—and, fixing our gaze on that location, any other location in that hemisphere rotates around it the same way. The traced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_path" title="Ground path">ground path</a> of a freely moving body travelling from one point to another therefore bends the opposite way, clockwise, which is conventionally labeled as "right," where it will be if the direction of motion is considered "ahead," and "down" is defined naturally.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotating_sphere">Rotating sphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Rotating sphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_coordinates.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="639" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Earth_coordinates.svg/220px-Earth_coordinates.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Earth_coordinates.svg/330px-Earth_coordinates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Earth_coordinates.svg/440px-Earth_coordinates.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth_coordinates.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coordinate system at latitude φ with <i>x</i>-axis east, <i>y</i>-axis north and <i>z</i>-axis upward (that is, radially outward from center of sphere).</div>
</div>
</div>

The rotation vector, velocity of movement and Coriolis acceleration expressed in this local coordinate system (listing components in the order east (<i>e</i>), north (<i>n</i>) and upward (<i>u</i>)) are:</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {a}}_{C}=-2{\boldsymbol {\Omega \times v}}=2\,\omega \,{\begin{pmatrix}v_{n}\sin \varphi -v_{u}\cos \varphi \\-v_{e}\sin \varphi \\v_{e}\cos \varphi \end{pmatrix}}\ .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\boldsymbol {a}}_{C}=-2{\boldsymbol {\Omega \times v}}=2\,\omega \,{\begin{pmatrix}v_{n}\sin \varphi -v_{u}\cos \varphi \\-v_{e}\sin \varphi \\v_{e}\cos \varphi \end{pmatrix}}\ ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f7582d2bb4b70e00ba33e1e6e5de94fba1eb5c0e" style="vertical-align: -4.171ex; width:46.223ex; height:9.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>

For such cases, only the horizontal (east and north) components matter. The restriction of the above to the horizontal plane is (setting <i>v<sub>u</sub></i> = 0):</p>

<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="f=2\omega \sin \varphi \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2154826491cca66019774e92fcb7bff188354d37" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:12.606ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> is called the Coriolis parameter.</p>
<p>By setting <i>v<sub>n</sub></i> = 0, it can be seen immediately that (for positive φ and ω) a movement due east results in an acceleration due south. Similarly, setting <i>v<sub>e</sub></i> = 0, it is seen that a movement due north results in an acceleration due east. In general, observed horizontally, looking along the direction of the movement causing the acceleration, the acceleration always is turned 90° to the right and of the same size regardless of the horizontal orientation.</p>

<p>Accordingly, an eastward motion (that is, in the same direction as the rotation of the sphere) provides an upward acceleration known as the <a href="/wiki/E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s_effect" title="Eötvös effect">Eötvös effect</a>, and an upward motion produces an acceleration due west.</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note"><span class="plainlinks selfreference noprint">For additional examples in this article see <a href="#Cannon_on_turntable">cannon on turntable</a> and <a href="#Tossed_ball_on_a_rotating_carousel">tossed ball</a>. In other articles, see <a href="/wiki/Rotating_spheres" title="Rotating spheres">rotating spheres</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force_(rotating_reference_frame)#Apparent_motion_of_stationary_objects" title="Centrifugal force (rotating reference frame)">apparent motion of stationary objects</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fictitious_force#Crossing_a_carousel" title="Fictitious force">carousel</a>.</span></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorology">Meteorology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Meteorology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Low_pressure_system_over_Iceland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5200" data-file-width="6000" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Low_pressure_system_over_Iceland.jpg/220px-Low_pressure_system_over_Iceland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Low_pressure_system_over_Iceland.jpg/330px-Low_pressure_system_over_Iceland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Low_pressure_system_over_Iceland.jpg/440px-Low_pressure_system_over_Iceland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

This <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low-pressure system</a> over <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> spins counterclockwise due to balance between the Coriolis force and the pressure gradient force.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coriolis_effect10.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="642" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Coriolis_effect10.svg/220px-Coriolis_effect10.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Coriolis_effect10.svg/330px-Coriolis_effect10.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Coriolis_effect10.svg/440px-Coriolis_effect10.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>

The pressure-gradient force is represented by blue arrows, the Coriolis acceleration (always perpendicular to the velocity) by red arrows</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coriolis_effect14.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="256" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Coriolis_effect14.png/220px-Coriolis_effect14.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/69/Coriolis_effect14.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3002" data-file-width="3000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/220px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/330px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/440px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cloud formations in a famous image of Earth from Apollo 17, makes similar circulation directly visible</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Perhaps the most important impact of the Coriolis effect is in the large-scale dynamics of the oceans and the atmosphere. In meteorology and <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>, it is convenient to postulate a rotating frame of reference wherein the Earth is stationary. In accommodation of that provisional postulation, the <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal</a> and Coriolis forces are introduced. Their relative importance is determined by the applicable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect#Length_scales_and_the_Rossby_number" title="Coriolis effect">Rossby numbers</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">Tornadoes</a> have high Rossby numbers, so, while tornado-associated centrifugal forces are quite substantial, Coriolis forces associated with tornadoes are for practical purposes negligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holton2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holton2-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because surface ocean currents are driven by the movement of wind over the water's surface, the Coriolis force also affects the movement of ocean currents and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">cyclones</a> as well. Many of the ocean's largest currents circulate around warm, high-pressure areas called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyre" title="Gyre">gyres</a>. Though the circulation is not as significant as that in the air, the deflection caused by the Coriolis effect is what creates the spiraling pattern in these gyres. The spiraling wind pattern helps the hurricane form. The stronger the force from the Coriolis effect, the faster the wind spins and picks up additional energy, increasing the strength of the hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coriolis_effect_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coriolis_effect-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Air within high-pressure systems rotates in a direction such that the Coriolis force is directed radially inwards, and nearly balanced by the outwardly radial pressure gradient. As a result, air travels clockwise around high pressure in the Northern Hemisphere and anticlockwise in the Southern Hemisphere. Air around low-pressure rotates in the opposite direction, so that the Coriolis force is directed radially outward and nearly balances an inwardly radial <a href="/wiki/Pressure_gradient" title="Pressure gradient">pressure gradient</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flow_around_a_low-pressure_area">Flow around a low-pressure area</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Flow around a low-pressure area">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">Low-pressure area</a></div>
<p>If a low-pressure area forms in the atmosphere, air tends to flow in towards it, but is deflected perpendicular to its velocity by the Coriolis force. A system of equilibrium can then establish itself creating circular movement, or a cyclonic flow. Because the Rossby number is low, the force balance is largely between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_gradient_force" title="Pressure gradient force">pressure gradient force</a> acting towards the low-pressure area and the Coriolis force acting away from the center of the low pressure.</p>
<p>Instead of flowing down the gradient, large scale motions in the atmosphere and ocean tend to occur perpendicular to the pressure gradient. This is known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geostrophic_flow" title="Geostrophic flow">geostrophic flow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barry_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barry-37">[37]</a></sup> On a non-rotating planet, fluid would flow along the straightest possible line, quickly eliminating pressure gradients. Note that the geostrophic balance is thus very different from the case of "inertial motions" (see below), which explains why mid-latitude cyclones are larger by an order of magnitude than inertial circle flow would be.</p>
<p>This pattern of deflection, and the direction of movement, is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buys-Ballot%27s_law" title="Buys-Ballot's law">Buys-Ballot's law</a>. In the atmosphere, the pattern of flow is called a <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclone</a>. In the Northern Hemisphere the direction of movement around a low-pressure area is anticlockwise. In the Southern Hemisphere, the direction of movement is clockwise because the rotational dynamics is a mirror image there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> At high altitudes, outward-spreading air rotates in the opposite direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Cyclones rarely form along the equator due to the weak Coriolis effect present in this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_circles">Inertial circles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Inertial circles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An air or water mass moving with speed <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>v</mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8b67d1fd725a759a151374b793113d7a78a65da4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.525ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> subject only to the Coriolis force travels in a circular trajectory called an 'inertial circle'. Since the force is directed at right angles to the motion of the particle, it moves with a constant speed around a circle whose radius <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>R</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle R}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="R" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b0bfb3769bf24d80e15374dc37b0441e2616e33" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.774ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is given by:</p>

The time taken for the mass to complete a full circle is therefore <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>f</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 2\pi /f}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="2\pi /f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/273e42883bc4e6bd4185b22235a3909e0364d943" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.977ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. The Coriolis parameter typically has a mid-latitude value of about 10<sup>−4</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>; hence for a typical atmospheric speed of 10 m/s (22 mph) the radius is 100 km (62 mi), with a period of about 17 hours. For an ocean current with a typical speed of 10 cm/s (0.22 mph), the radius of an inertial circle is 1 km (0.6 mi). These inertial circles are clockwise in the Northern Hemisphere (where trajectories are bent to the right) and anticlockwise in the Southern Hemisphere.</p>
<p>If the rotating system is a parabolic turntable, then <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/132e57acb643253e7810ee9702d9581f159a1c61" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.289ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is constant and the trajectories are exact circles. On a rotating planet, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</mstyle>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/132e57acb643253e7810ee9702d9581f159a1c61" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.289ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> varies with latitude and the paths of particles do not form exact circles. Since the parameter <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
