</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/132e57acb643253e7810ee9702d9581f159a1c61" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.289ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> varies as the sine of the latitude, the radius of the oscillations associated with a given speed are smallest at the poles (latitude = ±90°), and increase toward the equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall2_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall2-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_terrestrial_effects">Other terrestrial effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other terrestrial effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Coriolis effect strongly affects the large-scale oceanic and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">atmospheric circulation</a>, leading to the formation of robust features like <a href="/wiki/Jet_stream" title="Jet stream">jet streams</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_boundary_current" title="Western boundary current">western boundary currents</a>. Such features are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geostrophic" title="Geostrophic">geostrophic</a> balance, meaning that the Coriolis and <i>pressure gradient</i> forces balance each other. Coriolis acceleration is also responsible for the propagation of many types of waves in the ocean and atmosphere, including <a href="/wiki/Rossby_wave" title="Rossby wave">Rossby waves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_wave" title="Kelvin wave">Kelvin waves</a>. It is also instrumental in the so-called <a href="/wiki/Ekman_layer" title="Ekman layer">Ekman</a> dynamics in the ocean, and in the establishment of the large-scale ocean flow pattern called the <a href="/wiki/Sverdrup_balance" title="Sverdrup balance">Sverdrup balance</a>.</p>
<h3><span id="Eötvös_effect"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="E.C3.B6tv.C3.B6s_effect">Eötvös effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Eötvös effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s_effect" title="Eötvös effect">Eötvös effect</a></div>
<p>The practical impact of the "Coriolis effect" is mostly caused by the horizontal acceleration component produced by horizontal motion.</p>
<p>There are other components of the Coriolis effect. Westward-travelling objects are deflected downwards (feel heavier), while Eastward-travelling objects are deflected upwards (feel lighter).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> This is known as the <a href="/wiki/E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s_effect" title="Eötvös effect">Eötvös effect</a>. This aspect of the Coriolis effect is greatest near the equator. The force produced by this effect is similar to the horizontal component, but the much larger vertical forces due to gravity and pressure mean that it is generally unimportant dynamically.</p>
<p>In addition, objects travelling upwards (<i>i.e.</i>, out) or downwards (<i>i.e.</i>, in) are deflected to the west or east respectively. This effect is also the greatest near the equator. Since vertical movement is usually of limited extent and duration, the size of the effect is smaller and requires precise instruments to detect. However, in the case of large changes of momentum, such as a spacecraft being launched into orbit, the effect becomes significant. The fastest and most fuel-efficient path to orbit is a launch from the equator that curves to a directly eastward heading.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intuitive_example">Intuitive example</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Intuitive example">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Imagine a train that travels through a <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">frictionless</a> railway line along the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. Assume that, when in motion, it moves at the necessary speed to complete a trip around the world in one day (465 m/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persson_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Persson-43">[43]</a></sup> The Coriolis effect can be considered in three cases: when the train travels west, when it is at rest, and when it travels east. In each case, the Coriolis effect can be calculated from the <a href="/wiki/Rotating_reference_frame" title="Rotating reference frame">rotating frame of reference</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> first, and then checked against a fixed <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">inertial frame</a>. The image below illustrates the three cases in an inertial frame as observed from a fixed point above Earth along its <a href="/wiki/Rotation_around_a_fixed_axis" title="Rotation around a fixed axis">axis of rotation</a>:</p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_and_train_2FPS.gif" title="Earth and train"><img alt="Earth and train" data-file-height="170" data-file-width="480" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Earth_and_train_2FPS.gif" width="480"/></a></div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>1. The train travels toward the west: In that case, it moves against the direction of rotation. Therefore, on the Earth's rotating frame the Coriolis term is pointed inwards towards the axis of rotation (down). This additional force downwards should cause the train to be heavier while moving in that direction.
<ul>
<li>If one looks at this train from the fixed non-rotating frame on top of the center of the Earth, at that speed it remains stationary as the Earth spins beneath it. Hence, the only force acting on it is <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">gravity</a> and the reaction from the track. This force is greater (by 0.34%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persson_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Persson-43">[43]</a></sup> than the force that the passengers and the train experience when at rest (rotating along with Earth). This difference is what the Coriolis effect accounts for in the rotating frame of reference.</li>
</ul>
</dd>
<dd>2. The train comes to a stop: From the point of view on the Earth's rotating frame, the velocity of the train is zero, thus the Coriolis force is also zero and the train and its passengers recuperate their usual weight.
<ul>
<li>From the fixed inertial frame of reference above Earth, the train now rotates along with the rest of the Earth. 0.34% of the force of gravity provides the <a href="/wiki/Centripetal_force" title="Centripetal force">centripetal force</a> needed to achieve the circular motion on that frame of reference. The remaining force, as measured by a scale, makes the train and passengers "lighter" than in the previous case.</li>
</ul>
</dd>
<dd>3. The train travels east. In this case, because it moves in the direction of Earth's rotating frame, the Coriolis term is directed outward from the axis of rotation (up). This upward force makes the train seem lighter still than when at rest.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eotvos_efect_on_10Kg.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="737" data-file-width="1098" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Eotvos_efect_on_10Kg.png/350px-Eotvos_efect_on_10Kg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Eotvos_efect_on_10Kg.png/525px-Eotvos_efect_on_10Kg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Eotvos_efect_on_10Kg.png/700px-Eotvos_efect_on_10Kg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eotvos_efect_on_10Kg.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graph of the force experienced by a 10 kilograms object as a function of its speed moving along Earth's equator (as measured within the rotating frame). (Positive force in the graph is directed upward. Positive speed is directed eastward and negative speed is directed westward).</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>From the fixed inertial frame of reference above Earth, the train travelling east now rotates at twice the rate as when it was at rest—so the amount of centripetal force needed to cause that circular path increases leaving less force from gravity to act on the track. This is what the Coriolis term accounts for on the previous paragraph.</li>
<li>As a final check one can imagine a frame of reference rotating along with the train. Such frame would be rotating at twice the angular velocity as Earth's rotating frame. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> component for that imaginary frame would be greater. Since the train and its passengers are at rest, that would be the only component in that frame explaining again why the train and the passengers are lighter than in the previous two cases.</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>This also explains why high speed projectiles that travel west are deflected down, and those that travel east are deflected up. This vertical component of the Coriolis effect is called the <a href="/wiki/E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s_effect" title="Eötvös effect">Eötvös effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The above example can be used to explain why the Eötvös effect starts diminishing when an object is travelling westward as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangential_speed" title="Tangential speed">tangential speed</a> increases above Earth's rotation (465 m/s). If the westward train in the above example increases speed, part of the force of gravity that pushes against the track accounts for the centripetal force needed to keep it in circular motion on the inertial frame. Once the train doubles its westward speed at 930 m/s that centripetal force becomes equal to the force the train experiences when it stops. From the inertial frame, in both cases it rotates at the same speed but in the opposite directions. Thus, the force is the same cancelling completely the Eötvös effect. Any object that moves westward at a speed above 930 m/s experiences an upward force instead. In the figure, the Eötvös effect is illustrated for a 10 kilogram object on the train at different speeds. The parabolic shape is because the <a href="/wiki/Centripetal_force" title="Centripetal force">centripetal force</a> is proportional to the square of the tangential speed. On the inertial frame, the bottom of the parabola is centered at the origin. The offset is because this argument uses the Earth's rotating frame of reference. The graph shows that the Eötvös effect is not symmetrical, and that the resulting downward force experienced by an object that travels west at high velocity is less than the resulting upward force when it travels east at the same speed.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Draining_in_bathtubs_and_toilets">Draining in bathtubs and toilets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Draining in bathtubs and toilets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Contrary to popular misconception, water rotation in home bathrooms under <i>normal</i> circumstances is not related to the Coriolis effect or to the rotation of the Earth, and no consistent difference in rotation direction between toilet drainage in the Northern and Southern Hemispheres can be observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The formation of a vortex over the plug hole may be explained by the conservation of <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>: The radius of rotation decreases as water approaches the plug hole, so the rate of rotation increases, for the same reason that an ice skater's rate of spin increases as they pull their arms in. Any rotation around the plug hole that is initially present accelerates as water moves inward.</p>
<p>Of course, the Coriolis force does still impact the direction of the flow of water, but only minutely. Only if the water is so still that the effective rotation rate of the Earth is faster than that of the water relative to its container, and if externally applied torques (such as might be caused by flow over an uneven bottom surface) are small enough, the Coriolis effect may indeed determine the direction of the vortex. Without such careful preparation, the Coriolis effect is likely to be much smaller than various other influences on drain direction<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> such as any residual rotation of the water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and the geometry of the container.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Despite this, the idea that toilets and bathtubs drain differently in the Northern and Southern Hemispheres has been popularized by several television programs and films, including <i><a href="/wiki/Escape_Plan_(film)" title="Escape Plan (film)">Escape Plan</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Wedding_Crashers" title="Wedding Crashers">Wedding Crashers</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Simpsons" title="The Simpsons">The Simpsons</a></i> episode "<a href="/wiki/Bart_vs._Australia" title="Bart vs. Australia">Bart vs. Australia</a>", <i><a href="/wiki/Pole_to_Pole" title="Pole to Pole">Pole to Pole</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_X-Files" title="The X-Files">The X-Files</a></i> episode "<a href="/wiki/Die_Hand_Die_Verletzt" title="Die Hand Die Verletzt">Die Hand Die Verletzt</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Several science broadcasts and publications, including at least one college-level physics textbook, have also stated this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1908, the Austrian physicist <a href="/wiki/Ottokar_Tumlirz" title="Ottokar Tumlirz">Ottokar Tumlirz</a> described careful and effective experiments that demonstrated the effect of the rotation of the Earth on the outflow of water through a central aperture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The subject was later popularized in a famous 1962 article in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>, which described an experiment in which all other forces to the system were removed by filling a 6 ft (1.8 m) tank with 300 U.S. gal (1,100 L) of water and allowing it to settle for 24 hours (to allow any movement due to filling the tank to die away), in a room where the temperature had stabilized. The drain plug was then very slowly removed, and tiny pieces of floating wood were used to observe rotation. During the first 12 to 15 minutes, no rotation was observed. Then, a vortex appeared and consistently began to rotate in an anticlockwise direction (the experiment was performed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston,_Massachusetts" title="Boston, Massachusetts">Boston, Massachusetts</a>, in the Northern Hemisphere). This was repeated and the results averaged to make sure the effect was real. The report noted that the vortex rotated, "about 30,000 times faster than the effective rotation of the Earth in 42° North (the experiment's location)". This shows that the small initial rotation due to the Earth is amplified by gravitational draining and conservation of angular momentum to become a rapid vortex and may be observed under carefully controlled <a href="/wiki/Laboratory" title="Laboratory">laboratory</a> conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ballistic_trajectories">Ballistic trajectories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ballistic trajectories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Coriolis force is important in <a href="/wiki/External_ballistics#Coriolis_drift" title="External ballistics">external ballistics</a> for calculating the trajectories of very long-range <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> shells. The most famous historical example was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_gun" title="Paris gun">Paris gun</a>, used by the Germans during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> to bombard <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> from a range of about 120 km (75 mi). The Coriolis force minutely changes the trajectory of a bullet, affecting accuracy at extremely long distances. It is adjusted for by accurate long-distance shooters, such as snipers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor0_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor0-24">[24]</a></sup> Unlike large-scale motions of air in the atmosphere or water in the ocean, a bullet's path is not constrained to be horizontal, and the vertical component of the Coriolis force is often more important than the horizontal component: westward shots hit low, and eastward shots hit high.</p>
<p>The effects of the Coriolis force on ballistic trajectories should not be confused with the curvature of the paths of missiles, satellites, and similar objects when the paths are plotted on two-dimensional (flat) maps, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mercator_projection" title="Mercator projection">Mercator projection</a>. The projections of the three-dimensional curved surface of the Earth to a two-dimensional surface (the map) necessarily results in distorted features. The apparent curvature of the path is a consequence of the sphericity of the Earth and would occur even in a non-rotating frame.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Visualization_of_the_Coriolis_effect">Visualization of the Coriolis effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Visualization of the Coriolis effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parabola_shape_in_rotating_layers_of_fluid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="209" data-file-width="300" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Parabola_shape_in_rotating_layers_of_fluid.jpg/220px-Parabola_shape_in_rotating_layers_of_fluid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/Parabola_shape_in_rotating_layers_of_fluid.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forces_in_a_Parabolic_Dish.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="192" data-file-width="256" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Forces_in_a_Parabolic_Dish.svg/220px-Forces_in_a_Parabolic_Dish.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Forces_in_a_Parabolic_Dish.svg/330px-Forces_in_a_Parabolic_Dish.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Forces_in_a_Parabolic_Dish.svg/440px-Forces_in_a_Parabolic_Dish.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forces_in_a_Parabolic_Dish.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The forces at play in the case of a curved surface.<br/>
<i>Red</i>: gravity<br/>
<i>Green</i>: the <a href="/wiki/Normal_force" title="Normal force">normal force</a><br/>
<i>Blue</i>: the net resultant <a href="/wiki/Centripetal_force" title="Centripetal force">centripetal force</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><span id="parabolic_turntable"></span>To demonstrate the Coriolis effect, a parabolic turntable can be used. On a flat turntable, the inertia of a co-rotating object forces it off the edge. However, if the turntable surface has the correct <a href="/wiki/Paraboloid" title="Paraboloid">paraboloid</a> (parabolic bowl) shape (see the figure) and rotates at the corresponding rate, the force components shown in the figure make the component of gravity tangential to the bowl surface exactly equal to the centripetal force necessary to keep the object rotating at its velocity and radius of curvature (assuming no friction). (See <a href="/wiki/Centripetal_force#Example:_The_banked_turn" title="Centripetal force">banked turn</a>.) This carefully contoured surface allows the Coriolis force to be displayed in isolation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parabola_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-parabola-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-animation_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-animation-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Discs cut from cylinders of <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a> can be used as pucks, moving around almost frictionlessly over the surface of the parabolic turntable, allowing effects of Coriolis on dynamic phenomena to show themselves. To get a view of the motions as seen from the reference frame rotating with the turntable, a video camera is attached to the turntable so as to co-rotate with the turntable, with results as shown in the figure. In the left panel of the figure, which is the viewpoint of a stationary observer, the gravitational force in the inertial frame pulling the object toward the center (bottom ) of the dish is proportional to the distance of the object from the center. A centripetal force of this form causes the elliptical motion. In the right panel, which shows the viewpoint of the rotating frame, the inward gravitational force in the rotating frame (the same force as in the inertial frame) is balanced by the outward centrifugal force (present only in the rotating frame). With these two forces balanced, in the rotating frame the only unbalanced force is Coriolis (also present only in the rotating frame), and the motion is an <i><a href="#Inertial_circles">inertial circle</a></i>. Analysis and observation of circular motion in the rotating frame is a simplification compared to analysis or observation of elliptical motion in the inertial frame.</p>
<p>Because this reference frame rotates several times a minute rather than only once a day like the Earth, the Coriolis acceleration produced is many times larger and so easier to observe on small time and spatial scales than is the Coriolis acceleration caused by the rotation of the Earth.</p>
<p>In a manner of speaking, the Earth is analogous to such a turntable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall-62">[62]</a></sup> The rotation has caused the planet to settle on a spheroid shape, such that the normal force, the gravitational force and the centrifugal force exactly balance each other on a "horizontal" surface. (See <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_bulge" title="Equatorial bulge">equatorial bulge</a>.)</p>
<p>The Coriolis effect caused by the rotation of the Earth can be seen indirectly through the motion of a <a href="/wiki/Foucault_pendulum" title="Foucault pendulum">Foucault pendulum</a>.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coriolis_effects_in_other_areas">Coriolis effects in other areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Coriolis effects in other areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coriolis_flow_meter">Coriolis flow meter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Coriolis flow meter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A practical application of the Coriolis effect is the <a href="/wiki/Mass_flow_meter" title="Mass flow meter">mass flow meter</a>, an instrument that measures the <a href="/wiki/Mass_flow_rate" title="Mass flow rate">mass flow rate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of a fluid flowing through a tube. The operating principle involves inducing a vibration of the tube through which the fluid passes. The vibration, though not completely circular, provides the rotating reference frame that gives rise to the Coriolis effect. While specific methods vary according to the design of the flow meter, sensors monitor and analyze changes in frequency, phase shift, and amplitude of the vibrating flow tubes. The changes observed represent the mass flow rate and density of the fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_physics">Molecular physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Molecular physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In polyatomic molecules, the molecule motion can be described by a rigid body rotation and internal vibration of atoms about their equilibrium position. As a result of the vibrations of the atoms, the atoms are in motion relative to the rotating coordinate system of the molecule. Coriolis effects are therefore present, and make the atoms move in a direction perpendicular to the original oscillations. This leads to a mixing in molecular spectra between the rotational and vibrational <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">levels</a>, from which Coriolis coupling constants can be determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gyroscopic_precession">Gyroscopic precession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Gyroscopic precession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When an external torque is applied to a spinning gyroscope along an axis that is at right angles to the spin axis, the rim velocity that is associated with the spin becomes radially directed in relation to the external torque axis. This causes a Coriolis force to act on the rim in such a way as to tilt the gyroscope at right angles to the direction that the external torque would have tilted it. This tendency has the effect of keeping spinning bodies stably aligned in space.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insect_flight">Insect flight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Insect flight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Flies (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diptera" title="Diptera">Diptera</a>) and some moths (<a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a>) exploit the Coriolis effect in flight with specialized appendages and organs that relay information about the <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">angular velocity</a> of their bodies.</p>
<p>Coriolis forces resulting from linear motion of these appendages are detected within the rotating frame of reference of the insects' bodies. In the case of flies, their specialized appendages are dumbbell shaped organs located just behind their wings called "<a href="/wiki/Halteres" title="Halteres">halteres</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fly's halteres oscillate in a plane at the same beat frequency as the main wings so that any body rotation results in lateral deviation of the halteres from their plane of motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In moths, their antennae are known to be responsible for the <i>sensing</i> of Coriolis forces in the similar manner as with the halteres in flies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sane_S..2C_Dieudonn.C3.A9.2C_A..2C_Willis.2C_M..2C_Daniel.2C_T.date_.3D_February_2007_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sane_S..2C_Dieudonn.C3.A9.2C_A..2C_Willis.2C_M..2C_Daniel.2C_T.date_.3D_February_2007-67">[67]</a></sup> In both flies and moths, a collection of mechanosensors at the base of the appendage are sensitive to deviations at the beat frequency, correlating to rotation in the <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_principal_axes#Principal_axes" title="Aircraft principal axes">pitch and roll</a> planes, and at twice the beat frequency, correlating to rotation in the <a href="/wiki/Yaw_(rotation)" title="Yaw (rotation)">yaw</a> plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sane_S..2C_Dieudonn.C3.A9.2C_A..2C_Willis.2C_M..2C_Daniel.2C_T.date_.3D_February_2007_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sane_S..2C_Dieudonn.C3.A9.2C_A..2C_Willis.2C_M..2C_Daniel.2C_T.date_.3D_February_2007-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lagrangian_point_stability">Lagrangian point stability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Coriolis_force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Lagrangian point stability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In astronomy, <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a> are five positions in the orbital plane of two large orbiting bodies where a small object affected only by gravity can maintain a stable position relative to the two large bodies. The first three Lagrangian points (L<sub>1</sub>, L<sub>2</sub>, L<sub>3</sub>) lie along the line connecting the two large bodies, while the last two points (L<sub>4</sub> and L<sub>5</sub>) each form an equilateral triangle with the two large bodies. The L<sub>4</sub> and L<sub>5</sub> points, although they correspond to maxima of the <a href="/wiki/Effective_potential" title="Effective potential">effective potential</a> in the coordinate frame that rotates with the two large bodies, are stable due to the Coriolis effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The stability can result in orbits around just L<sub>4</sub> or L<sub>5</sub>, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tadpole_orbit" title="Tadpole orbit">Tadpole orbits</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Trojan_(astronomy)" title="Trojan (astronomy)">trojans</a> can be found. It can also result in orbits that encircle L<sub>3</sub>, L<sub>4</sub>, and L<sub>5</sub>, known as <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_orbit" title="Horseshoe orbit">horseshoe orbits</a>.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<li id="cite_note-caution-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-caution_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Here the description "radially inward" means "toward the axis of rotation". That direction is <i>not</i> toward the <a href="/wiki/Osculating_circle" title="Osculating circle">center of curvature</a> of the path, however, which is the direction of the true centripetal force. Hence, the quotation marks on "centripetal".</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-parabola-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-parabola_60-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When a container of fluid is rotating on a turntable, the surface of the fluid naturally assumes the correct <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabolic</a> shape. This fact may be exploited to make a parabolic turntable by using a fluid that sets after several hours, such as a synthetic <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resin</a>. For a video of the Coriolis effect on such a parabolic surface, see <a class="external text" href="http://www-paoc.mit.edu/labweb/lab5/gfd_v.htm" rel="nofollow">Geophysical fluid dynamics lab demonstration</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20051120121513/http://www-paoc.mit.edu/labweb/lab5/gfd_v.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 20 November 2005 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. John Marshall, Massachusetts Institute of Technology.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-animation-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-animation_61-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a java applet of the Coriolis effect on such a parabolic surface, see <a class="external text" href="http://mensch.org/physlets/inosc.html" rel="nofollow">Brian Fiedler</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060321094211/http://www.mensch.org/physlets/inosc.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 21 March 2006 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. School of Meteorology at the University of Oklahoma.</span></li>

<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mcPs_OdQOYU" rel="nofollow"><i>Coriolis Effect: A graphical animation</i></a>, a visual Earth animation with precise explanation</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=khRil3tWYEA" rel="nofollow"><i>An introduction to fluid dynamics</i></a> SPINLab Educational Film explains the Coriolis effect with the aid of lab experiments</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.straightdope.com/classics/a1_161.html" rel="nofollow"><i>Do bathtubs drain counterclockwise in the Northern Hemisphere?</i></a> by Cecil Adams.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.ems.psu.edu/~fraser/Bad/BadCoriolis.html" rel="nofollow"><i>Bad Coriolis.</i></a> An article uncovering misinformation about the Coriolis effect. By Alistair B. Fraser, Emeritus Professor of Meteorology at <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_University" title="Pennsylvania State University">Pennsylvania State University</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://stratus.ssec.wisc.edu/courses/gg101/coriolis/coriolis.html" rel="nofollow"><i>The Coriolis Effect: A (Fairly) Simple Explanation</i></a>, an explanation for the layperson</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.classzone.com/books/earth_science/terc/content/visualizations/es1904/es1904page01.cfm?chapter_no=visualization" rel="nofollow">Observe an animation of the Coriolis effect over Earth's surface</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=49JwbrXcPjc" rel="nofollow">Animation clip</a> showing scenes as viewed from both an inertial frame and a rotating frame of reference, visualizing the Coriolis and centrifugal forces.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.inwit.com/inwit/writings/coriolisforce.html" rel="nofollow">Vincent Mallette <i>The Coriolis Force</i> @ INWIT</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://pwg.gsfc.nasa.gov/stargaze/Srotfram.htm" rel="nofollow">NASA notes</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://andygiger.com/science/e-coriolis/index.html" rel="nofollow">Interactive Coriolis Fountain</a> lets you control rotation speed, droplet speed and frame of reference to explore the Coriolis effect.</li>
</ul>
